- Created: 2026-04-28T08:48:39Z
- Last modified: 2026-04-28T09:46:47Z

### USER Objective:
Integrating Remote ESP-NOW Network

The user's objective is to establish a three-node ESP-NOW wireless network for an electric mountain board project. The project involves: 
1. Configuring a Lilygo T-Display S3 as a remote controller (potentiometer and buttons) to send throttle commands.
2. Setting up an ESP8266MOD as a receiver to bridge these commands to an ESC via UART using the Flipsky FTESC protocol.
3. Updating the existing Waveshare ESP32-S3 dashboard to act as a telemetry hub, sending live CAN data to the remote via ESP-NOW.
4. Implementing a simulation environment (`native_remote` and `native_dash`) for testing and ensuring the cross-board communication logic is functional across hardware and software environments.

</conversation_summaries>

111:     // Assume 2048 is neutral. Deadband around neutral.
112:     float throttle = 0.0f;
113:     if (pot_val > 2148) {
114:         throttle = ((pot_val - 2148) / (4095.0f - 2148.0f)) * 100.0f;
115:     } else if (pot_val < 1948) {
116:         throttle = ((pot_val - 1948) / 1948.0f) * 100.0f; // Negative
117:     }
118: 
119:     // Send every 50ms (20Hz)
120:     if (millis() - last_send > 50) {
121:         last_send = millis();
122:         ControlPacket pkt;
123:         pkt.throttle_percent = throttle;

The following code has been modified to include a line number before every line, in the format: <line_number>: <original_line>. Please note that any changes targeting the original code should remove the line number, colon, and leading space.
1: #include <Arduino.h>
2: #include "../espnow_packets.h"
3: 
4: #ifdef ARDUINO
5: #include <ESP8266WiFi.h>
6: #include <espnow.h>
7: 
8: // MAC Addresses
9: // Remote: D0:CF:13:32:42:3C
10: static uint8_t remote_mac[] = {0xD0, 0xCF, 0x13, 0x32, 0x42, 0x3C};
11: 
12: static float current_throttle = 0.0f;
13: static uint32_t last_packet_ms = 0;
14: 
15: // UART logic
16: void sendVescUartDuty(float dutyCycle) {
17:     // Basic VESC UART Payload for COMM_SET_DUTY
18:     // 0x02, payload_len, COMM_SET_DUTY (5), duty (4 bytes int32 * 100000), CRC (2 bytes), 0x03
19:     int32_t val = (int32_t)(dutyCycle * 100000.0f);
20:     
21:     uint8_t payload[5];
22:     payload[0] = 5; // COMM_SET_DUTY
23:     payload[1] = (val >> 24) & 0xFF;
24:     payload[2] = (val >> 16) & 0xFF;
25:     payload[3] = (val >> 8) & 0xFF;
26:     payload[4] = val & 0xFF;
27:     
28:     // Calculate CRC (CRC-16-CCITT)
29:     uint16_t crc = 0;
30:     for (int i = 0; i < 5; i++) {
31:         crc = crc ^ (payload[i] << 8);
32:         for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) {
33:             if (crc & 0x8000) crc = (crc << 1) ^ 0x1021;
34:             else crc <<= 1;
35:         }
36:     }
37:     
38:     // Send
39:     Serial.write(0x02);
40:     Serial.write(5); // len
41:     Serial.write(payload, 5);
42:     Serial.write((crc >> 8) & 0xFF);
43:     Serial.write(crc & 0xFF);
44:     Serial.write(0x03);
45: }
46: 
47: // Receive callback for ESP8266 ESP-NOW
48: void onDataRecv(uint8_t * mac, uint8_t *incomingData, uint8_t len) {
49:     if (len == sizeof(ControlPacket)) {
50:         ControlPacket pkt;
51:         memcpy(&pkt, incomingData, sizeof(ControlPacket));
52:         current_throttle = pkt.throttle_percent;
53:         last_packet_ms = millis();
54: 
55: #ifdef DEBUG_ESPNOW
56:         Serial.printf("[ESP-NOW] RX Remote -> Receiver | Throttle: %.1f%%\n", current_throttle);
57: #endif
58:     }
59: }
60: 
61: void setup() {
62:     // Serial connected to ESC
63:     Serial.begin(115200);
64: 
65:     WiFi.mode(WIFI_STA);
66:     if (esp_now_init() != 0) {
67:         return;
68:     }
69:     esp_now_set_self_role(ESP_NOW_ROLE_SLAVE);
70:     esp_now_register_recv_cb(onDataRecv);
71: }
72: 
73: void loop() {
74:     // Failsafe
75:     if (millis() - last_packet_ms > 500) {
76:         current_throttle = 0.0f; // Neutral
77:     }
78:     
79:     // Map throttle (-100 to 100) to Duty Cycle (-0.95 to 0.95)
80:     float duty = current_throttle / 100.0f * 0.95f;
81:     
82:     // Send to ESC every 50ms
83:     static uint32_t last_uart = 0;
84:     if (millis() - last_uart > 50) {
85:         last_uart = millis();
86:         sendVescUartDuty(duty);
87: #ifdef DEBUG_ESPNOW
88:         Serial.printf("[UART] TX Receiver -> ESC | VESC COMM_SET_DUTY: %.3f\n", duty);
89: #endif
90:     }
91: }
92: #endif
93: 
The above content shows the entire, complete file contents of the requested file.

+    int tap_count = 0;
+    uint32_t last_tap_time = 0;

+    static void top_right_event_cb(lv_event_t * e) {
+        uint32_t t = lv_tick_get();
+        if (t - last_tap_time > 1000) {
+            tap_count = 0;
+        }
+        tap_count++;
+        last_tap_time = t;
+        if (tap_count >= 3) {
+            debug_mode_active = !debug_mode_active;
+            if (debug_mode_active) {
+                lv_scr_load(debug_screen);
+            } else {
+                lv_scr_load(main_screen);
+            }
+            tap_count = 0;
+        }
+    }

31:     int32_t val = (int32_t)(dutyCycle * 100000.0f);
32:     
33:     uint8_t payload[5];
34:     payload[0] = 5; // COMM_SET_DUTY
35:     payload[1] = (val >> 24) & 0xFF;
36:     payload[2] = (val >> 16) & 0xFF;
37:     payload[3] = (val >> 8) & 0xFF;
38:     payload[4] = val & 0xFF;
39:     
40:     // Calculate CRC (CRC-16-CCITT)
41:     uint16_t crc = 0;
42:     for (int i = 0; i < 5; i++) {
43:         crc = crc ^ (payload[i] << 8);
44:         for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) {
45:             if (crc & 0x8000) crc = (crc << 1) ^ 0x1021;
46:             else crc <<= 1;
47:         }
48:     }

50:     // Send
51:     Serial.

### USER Objective:
Integrating Remote ESP-NOW Network

The user's objective is to establish a three-node ESP-NOW wireless network for an electric mountain board project. The project involves: 
1. Configuring a Lilygo T-Display S3 as a remote controller (potentiometer and buttons) to send throttle commands.
2. Setting up an ESP8266MOD as a receiver to bridge these commands to an ESC via UART using the Flipsky FTESC protocol.
3. Updating the existing Waveshare ESP32-S3 dashboard to act as a telemetry hub, sending live CAN data to the remote via ESP-NOW.
4. Implementing a simulation environment (`native_remote` and `native_dash`) for testing and ensuring the cross-board communication logic is functional across hardware and software environments.

=== Step 344 ===
CONTENT: Based on the source code in [receiver_app.cpp](file:///c:/Users/thatw/Documents/Apollo-8/DashBoard/src/receiver/receiver_app.cpp), here is how the receiver behaves via UART, including the specific commands sent, their structures, and their functionality.

---

### 1. UART Settings & Periodic Behavior
* **Baud Rate:** `115200` (initialized in `ReceiverApp::init()`).
* **Periodicity:** The receiver sends control commands to the ESC every **50ms** (`last_uart` timer).
* **Failsafe:** If the receiver stops receiving ESP-NOW control packets from the remote for more than **500ms**, `current_throttle` is automatically reset to `0.0` (neutral) to stop the board.

---

### 2. Standard Operation: Commands Sent
Under normal operation, the receiver sends only **one** binary command to the VESC ESC to control the motor speed:

#### **`COMM_SET_DUTY` (Command ID: `5`)**
* **Purpose:** Sets the target duty cycle (motor speed/power percentage) of the ESC.
* **Input Mapping:** 
  1. Maps the remote's throttle percent ($-100.0\%$ to $+100.0\%$) to a duty cycle fraction ($-0.95$ to $+0.95$):
     $$\text{duty} = \frac{\text{Throttle \%}}{100.0} \times 0.95$$
  2. Scales the floating-point duty cycle to a 32-bit signed big-endian integer by multiplying by `100,000`:
     $$\text{val} = \text{duty} \times 100000$$
     *(e.g., maximum forward throttle $+100\%$ maps to a payload value of $+95,000$)*

#### **UART Packet Structure (Standard VESC Framing):**
The packet is transmitted as a sequential stream of 9 bytes:

| Byte Index | Field Name | Hex / Formula | Description |
| :--- | :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **0** | **Start Byte** | `0x02` | VESC start frame marker for payload size $\le 256$ bytes |
| **1** | **Payload Length** | `0x05` | Payload length is exactly 5 bytes |
| **2** | **Command ID** | `0x05` | `COMM_SET_DUTY` VESC command |
| **3** | **Data MSB** | `(val >> 24) & 0xFF` | Most significant byte of the 32-bit duty cycle integer |
| **4** | **Data Byte 2** | `(val >> 16) & 0xFF` | Second byte |
| **5** | **Data Byte 3** | `(val >> 8) & 0xFF` | Third byte |
| **6** | **Data LSB** | `val & 0xFF` | Least significant byte |
| **7** | **CRC MSB** | `(crc >> 8) & 0xFF` | CRC-16-CCITT (polynomial `0x1021`, initial `0`) calculated over bytes 2â€“6 |
| **8** | **CRC LSB** | `crc & 0xFF` | LSB of the calculated CRC-16 |
| **9** | **End Byte** | `0x03` | VESC end frame marker |

---

### 3. Debug & Mocking Mode Behavior (`RECEIVER_DEBUG_MODE`)
If compile-time flag `RECEIVER_DEBUG_MODE` is enabled on hardware:
1. **Instead of sending binary packets**, the receiver outputs human-readable text via UART at the same 50ms frequency:
   `[DEBUG] ESC Duty: <float_value>`
2. **UART RX Commands (Console Mocking):**
   The receiver listens on its serial interface for incoming text commands from a PC to mock the ESC telemetry:
   * **Command:** `telemetry speed <float_value>`
   * **Action:** Parses the value, generates a `TelemetryPacket`, and transmits it wirelessly via ESP-NOW to the Dashboard (`dash_mac`).

### What is currently implemented:

   ```text
   Speed: <speed>
   Batt: <voltage>

---

### What a "Proper" LilyGo ($320 \times 170$) UI should have:
To match high-end electric skateboard remotes (like the Flipsky or Hoyt St), it needs:
* **Circular Speed Gauge / Arc:** Visual indication of current speed.

1: ---
2: name: brainstorming
3: description: "You MUST use this before any creative work - creating features, building components, adding functionality, or modifying behavior. Explores user intent, requirements and design before implementation."
4: ---
5: 
6: # Brainstorming Ideas Into Designs
7: 
8: Help turn ideas into fully formed designs and specs through natural collaborative dialogue.
9: 
10: Start by understanding the current project context, then ask questions one at a time to refine the idea. Once you understand what you're building, present the design and get user approval.
11: 
12: <HARD-GATE>
13: Do NOT invoke any implementation skill, write any code, scaffold any project, or take any implementation action until you have presented a design and the user has approved it. This applies to EVERY project regardless of perceived simplicity.
14: </HARD-GATE>
15: 
16: ## Anti-Pattern: "This Is Too Simple To Need A Design"
17: 
18: Every project goes through this process. A todo list, a single-function utility, a config change â€” all of them. "Simple" projects are where unexamined assumptions cause the most wasted work. The design can be short (a few sentences for truly simple projects), but you MUST present it and get approval.
19: 
20: ## Checklist
21: 
22: You MUST create a task for each of these items and complete them in order:
23: 
24: 1. **Explore project context** â€” check files, docs, recent commits
25: 2. **Offer visual companion** (if topic will involve visual questions) â€” this is its own message, not combined with a clarifying question. See the Visual C
<truncated 7788 bytes>
browser-based companion for showing mockups, diagrams, and visual options during brainstorming. Available as a tool â€” not a mode. Accepting the companion means it's available for questions that benefit from visual treatment; it does NOT mean every question goes through the browser.
150: 
151: **Offering the companion:** When you anticipate that upcoming questions will involve visual content (mockups, layouts, diagrams), offer it once for consent:
152: > "Some of what we're working on might be easier to explain if I can show it to you in a web browser. I can put together mockups, diagrams, comparisons, and other visuals as we go. This feature is still new and can be token-intensive. Want to try it? (Requires opening a local URL)"
153: 
154: **This offer MUST be its own message.** Do not combine it with clarifying questions, context summaries, or any other content. The message should contain ONLY the offer above and nothing else. Wait for the user's response before continuing. If they decline, proceed with text-only brainstorming.
155: 
156: **Per-question decision:** Even after the user accepts, decide FOR EACH QUESTION whether to use the browser or the terminal. The test: **would the user understand this better by seeing it than reading it?**
157: 
158: - **Use the browser** for content that IS visual â€” mockups, wireframes, layout comparisons, architecture diagrams, side-by-side visual designs
159: - **Use the terminal** for content that is text â€” requirements questions, conceptual choices, tradeoff lists, A/B/C/D text options, scope decisions
160: 
161: A question about a UI topic is not automatically a visual question. "What does personality mean in this context?" is a conceptual question â€” use the terminal. "Which wizard layout works better?" is a visual question â€” use the browser.
162: 
163: If they agree to the companion, read the detailed guide before proceeding:
164: `skills/brainstorming/visual-companion.md`
165:

The following code has been modified to include a line number before every line, in the format: <line_number>: <original_line>. Please note that any changes targeting the original code should remove the line number, colon, and leading space.
1: # Visual Companion Guide
2: 
3: Browser-based visual brainstorming companion for showing mockups, diagrams, and options.
4: 
5: ## When to Use
6: 
7: Decide per-question, not per-session. The test: **would the user understand this better by seeing it than reading it?**
8: 
9: **Use the browser** when the content itself is visual:
10: 
11: - **UI mockups** â€” wireframes, layouts, navigation structures, component designs
12: - **Architecture diagrams** â€” system components, data flow, relationship maps
13: - **Side-by-side visual comparisons** â€” comparing two layouts, two color schemes, two design directions
14: - **Design polish** â€” when the question is about look and feel, spacing, visual hierarchy
15: - **Spatial relationships** â€” state machines, flowcharts, entity relationships rendered as diagrams
16: 
17: **Use the terminal** when the content is text or tabular:
18: 
19: - **Requirements and scope questions** â€” "what does X mean?", "which features are in scope?"
20: - **Conceptual A/B/C choices** â€” picking between approaches described in words
21: - **Tradeoff lists** â€” pros/cons, comparison tables
22: - **Technical decisions** â€” API design, data modeling, architectural approach selection
23: - **Clarifying questions** â€” anything where the answer is words, not a visual preference
24: 
25: A question *about* a UI topic is not automatically a visual question. "What kind of wizard do you want?" is conceptual â€” use the terminal. "Which of these wizard layouts feels right?" is visual â€” use the browser.
26: 
27: ## How It Works
28: 
29: T
<truncated 9827 bytes>
ption A - Simple Layout","timestamp":1706000101}
252: {"type":"click","choice":"c","text":"Option C - Complex Grid","timestamp":1706000108}
253: {"type":"click","choice":"b","text":"Option B - Hybrid","timestamp":1706000115}
254: ```
255: 
256: The full event stream shows the user's exploration path â€” they may click multiple options before settling. The last `choice` event is typically the final selection, but the pattern of clicks can reveal hesitation or preferences worth asking about.
257: 
258: If `$STATE_DIR/events` doesn't exist, the user didn't interact with the browser â€” use only their terminal text.
259: 
260: ## Design Tips
261: 
262: - **Scale fidelity to the question** â€” wireframes for layout, polish for polish questions
263: - **Explain the question on each page** â€” "Which layout feels more professional?" not just "Pick one"
264: - **Iterate before advancing** â€” if feedback changes current screen, write a new version
265: - **2-4 options max** per screen
266: - **Use real content when it matters** â€” for a photography portfolio, use actual images (Unsplash). Placeholder content obscures design issues.
267: - **Keep mockups simple** â€” focus on layout and structure, not pixel-perfect design
268: 
269: ## File Naming
270: 
271: - Use semantic names: `platform.html`, `visual-style.html`, `layout.html`
272: - Never reuse filenames â€” each screen must be a new file
273: - For iterations: append version suffix like `layout-v2.html`, `layout-v3.html`
274: - Server serves newest file by modification time
275: 
276: ## Cleaning Up
277: 
278: ```bash
279: scripts/stop-server.sh $SESSION_DIR
280: ```
281: 
282: If the session used `--project-dir`, mockup files persist in `.superpowers/brainstorm/` for later reference. Only `/tmp` sessions get deleted on stop.
283: 
284: ## Reference
285: 
286: - Frame template (CSS reference): `scripts/frame-template.html`
287: - Helper script (client-side): `scripts/helper.js`
288: 
The above content shows the entire, complete file contents of the requested file.

6: # Writing Plans
7: 
8: ## Overview
9: 
10: Write comprehensive implementation plans assuming the engineer has zero context for our codebase and questionable taste. 19: - (User preferences for plan location override this default)
20:

22: 
23: If the spec covers multiple independent subsystems, it should have been broken into sub-project specs during brainstorming. If it wasn't, suggest breaking this into separate plans â€” one per subsystem. Each plan should produce working, testable software on its own.
24:

1: # Multi-Window Digital Test Environment
2: 
3: This implementation plan outlines the architectural changes required to expand the `native_full_stack` simulation from a single window to a three-window setup (Dash, Remote, and Receiver), complete with interactive UI widgets to mock physical hardware.
4: 
5: ## User Review Required
6: 
7: > [!IMPORTANT]
8: > The current `RemoteApp` uses `TFT_eSPI` for its display on the physical hardware. To simulate it in a native window with interactive widgets (sliders for the potentiometer, on-screen buttons for physical buttons), we will convert the `RemoteApp` to use **LVGL** instead of `TFT_eSPI`. This will make the Remote UI look much better on the physical hardware as well, and it standardizes the UI framework across all three devices. Is this acceptable?
9: 
10: ## Open Questions
11: 
12: > [!WARNING]
13: > For the Receiver's UI in the simulation, it will display the incoming throttle from the Remote. Do you also want interactive sliders on the Receiver's simulation window to easily mock the ESC telemetry (Speed, Battery, Power) being sent back to the Dash, replacing the current serial-text-based mocking? (My plan below assumes YES).
14: 
15: ## Proposed Changes
16: 
17: ---
18: 
19: ### Custom Multi-Window SDL Wrapper
20: 
21: The default LVGL SDL driver only supports a single window. We will bypass it in the full stack simulation and write a custom multi-window driver.
22: 
23: #### [MODIFY] [sim_main.cpp](file:///c:/Users/thatw/Documents/Apollo-8/DashBoard/src/simulation/sim_main.cpp)
24:
<truncated 578 bytes>
hatw/Documents/Apollo-8/DashBoard/src/display_driver.cpp)
30: - Disable the default `sdl_init()` and LVGL registration inside `DisplayDriver::init()` when `NATIVE_FULL_STACK` is defined, as `sim_main.cpp` will handle it.
31: 
32: ---
33: 
34: ### Remote UI & Hardware Simulation
35: 
36: #### [MODIFY] [RemoteApp](file:///c:/Users/thatw/Documents/Apollo-8/DashBoard/src/remote/remote_app.cpp)
37: - **Refactor**: Remove `TFT_eSPI` and implement the UI using LVGL.
38: - **Native Widgets**: When compiled for `NATIVE_FULL_STACK`, the `init()` method will render an LVGL slider (to act as the potentiometer) and on-screen buttons (to act as the physical buttons).
39: - **Logic**: `update()` will read from the LVGL slider to calculate throttle when running in the simulation, instead of relying on the previously hardcoded `sim_pot_val`.
40: 
41: ---
42: 
43: ### Receiver UI
44: 
45: #### [MODIFY] [ReceiverApp](file:///c:/Users/thatw/Documents/Apollo-8/DashBoard/src/receiver/receiver_app.cpp)
46: - **Simulation UI**: When compiled for `NATIVE_FULL_STACK`, `init()` will create a basic LVGL UI containing:
47:   1. A text label showing the received `current_throttle` from the remote.
48:   2. A slider to mock ESC Speed (km/h).
49:   3. A slider to mock ESC Battery (V).
50:   4. A slider to mock ESC Power (W).
51: - **Logic**: `update()` will read the values from these sliders and use them to populate the mock `TelemetryPacket` sent back to the Dash, replacing the USB Serial parsing.
52:

62: - Drag the Speed/Battery sliders on the Receiver window and verify the Dashboard UI updates accordingly.

1: # Multi-Window Simulation & Remote UI Upgrade

8:     - Each window gets its own `lv_disp_t` and `lv_indev_t`, allowing independent rendering and mouse interaction within the same process.
9: 2.  **Remote App UI Upgrade:** 
10:     - Converted the Remote app from `TFT_eSPI` printing to a fully-fledged LVGL UI. This significantly improves aesthetics on the physical board and unifies the UI framework.
11:     - Added an interactive LVGL slider (`slider_pot`) in the simulation to mock the physical potentiometer. You can drag it to set the throttle!
12: 3.  **Receiver App Simulation UI:** 
13:     - Added a custom LVGL UI that only compiles for the simulation.
14:     - It displays the raw `RX Throttle` received from the remote.
15:     - Added three interactive LVGL sliders (Speed, Battery, Power) to mock the ESC telemetry being sent back to the Dash, replacing the old text-based serial mock.

28:     ```

31:     - **Receiver Window:** Watch the throttle update, and drag the Speed/Batt/Power sliders to send ESC telemetry back to the dashboard.

53: - **Lilygo (Remote)**: `D0:CF:13:32:42:3C`
54: - **Receiver (ESP8266)**: `EC:64:C9:CC:D8:54`

=== Step 592 ===
CONTENT: To connect your **ESP8266 Receiver (D1 Mini / ESP8266MOD)** to the **VESC ESC** via UART, make the following pin connections:

| ESP8266 Receiver Pin | VESC ESC UART Pin | Description |
| :--- | :--- | :--- |
| **`TX`** (GPIO 1) | **`RX`** | Transmits the VESC duty cycle packets to the ESC |
| **`RX`** (GPIO 3) | **`TX`** | (Optional) Receives telemetry from the ESC |
| **`GND`** | **`GND`** | **CRITICAL:** Common ground reference (must connect!) |
| **`5V`** / **`5V-In`** | **`5V`** | Powers the receiver board directly from the ESC |

### Important Guidelines:
1. **Cross TX and RX:** Always connect `TX` to `RX` and `RX` to `TX`.
2. **Baud Rate:** The receiver is programmed to communicate at **`115200`** baud. Make sure the VESC tool configuration for the ESC's UART port is set to **`115200`** and the App Mode is set to **`UART`** or **`PPM and UART`**.

=== Step 600 ===
CONTENT: To configure your VESC to work with the receiver over UART, you need to adjust a few settings in the **VESC Tool**. Connect your VESC to your computer, open the VESC Tool, and set the following:

1. **App to Use**: Go to *App Settings* > *General* and set **App to Use** to **UART** (or **PPM and UART** if you plan to use both).
2. **UART Baud Rate**: Go to *App Settings* > *UART* and set the **Baud Rate** to **115200 bps**. (This matches the `115200` baud rate configured in your receiver's `receiver_app.cpp` code).
3. **Control Type / Timeout**: Ensure the VESC is allowed to accept control commands over UART. The receiver code explicitly sends `COMM_SET_DUTY` commands, which the VESC natively understands as a Duty Cycle control signal.

Once these are set, make sure your pins are crossed properly as well:
* Receiver **TX** âž” VESC **RX**
* Receiver **RX** âž” VESC **TX**
* Receiver **GND** âž” VESC **GND**

Write the app configuration to the VESC and reboot it. It should start responding to the throttle inputs from the remote! Let me know if you want to jump back into the UI mockups.

=== Step 613 ===
CONTENT: I've created a detailed safety analysis. Here are the **critical takeaways**:

### ðŸ”´ The Big Problem
Your receiver sends **`COMM_SET_DUTY`** (raw duty cycle), but your ESC is configured in **"Current Gear Reverse Brake"** mode (current-based control). `COMM_SET_DUTY` is a low-level command that **bypasses** the ESC's app layer â€” meaning all those nice safety settings you configured (Slow-Release Motor, Brake-Parking, current limiting) are likely **completely ignored**.

### ðŸ”´ No Ramp Protection
The code maps throttle to duty **instantly** â€” `duty = throttle / 100 * 0.95`. A sudden flick of the potentiometer = an instant jump to 95% duty = violent lurch or sudden stop.

### ðŸŸ  Failsafe = Instant Lock-Up Risk
Signal loss â†’ instant `duty = 0` â†’ with Brake-Parking ON, the motor could lock at speed, throwing the rider.

### The Fix
**Switch from `COMM_SET_DUTY` (ID 5) to `COMM_SET_CURRENT` (ID 6)** in the receiver firmware. This single change makes all your ESC safety features actually work. Then add a software ramp limiter and a gradual failsafe ramp-down.

Check out the full analysis artifact for details. Want me to implement the fixes?


=== Step 615 ===
CONTENT: A few more I'd flag:

### ðŸ”´ No Input Validation on Received Throttle
The receiver blindly trusts whatever value arrives in the `ControlPacket`:
```cpp
current_throttle = pkt.throttle_percent;  // Line 74 â€” no bounds check
```
If the packet is corrupted over ESP-NOW, or the remote's ADC glitches, `throttle_percent` could be **any float** â€” e.g. `10000.0` â†’ duty of `95.0` (way beyond 1.0). The VESC would clamp it, but you should **never** rely on the ESC for input safety. Add a `constrain(-100.0, 100.0)` clamp.

### ðŸŸ  Reverse Delay Probably Bypassed
Your ESC has `Reverse Delay Time: 5.0s` â€” a great safety feature. But since `COMM_SET_DUTY` bypasses the app layer, a negative duty (reverse throttle) would likely apply **instantly** with no delay. At speed, instant reverse = instant lock-up.

### ðŸŸ  No Watchdog Timer
If the ESP8266 crashes or hangs mid-ride, the UART stops sending. The ESC *might* have its own timeout, or it might hold the last received duty forever. A hardware watchdog that auto-resets the MCU would prevent a stuck-throttle scenario.

### ðŸŸ¡ No Deadzone Around Zero Throttle
Potentiometers drift. If the center position reads `0.3%` instead of `0.0%`, the board will **creep forward** when you think it's neutral. A Â±2-3% deadzone is standard.

### ðŸŸ¡ ESP-NOW Is Unencrypted
The ESP-NOW link has no encryption or authentication. Anyone within ~200m with the right MAC address could inject `ControlPacket`s and take control of your board. Low probability in practice, but worth knowing.

---

All of these get fixed naturally when we implement the receiver safety update. Want me to build the implementation plan covering all of these?

### âœ… Implemented Changes

| Fix | What changed |
|-----|-------------|
| **COMM_SET_DUTY â†’ COMM_SET_CURRENT** | New generic `sendVescUartCommand()` sends command ID 6 (drive) or 7 (brake) instead of ID 5 (duty) |
| **Ramp limiter** | New `ramped_throttle` variable smoothly tracks `current_throttle` at max 75%/sec (~1.3s 0â†’100%) |
| **Coast failsafe** | Signal loss â†’ target ramps to 0 at 60%/sec, then sends `COMM_SET_CURRENT(0)` = **coast, not brake** |
| **Input clamping** | Received throttle clamped to Â±100% in the ESP-NOW callback |
| **Deadzone** | Â±3% around center = coast |
| **Negative = regen brake** | Pulling throttle back sends `COMM_SET_CURRENT_BRAKE`, **never reverse** |

=== Step 665 ===
CONTENT: Good news â€” ESP-NOW link is working! The problem is between the receiver and the ESC. Most likely culprit:

### Are you still running `receiver_d1_mini_debug`?

That's the build you just flashed to verify receiving. In debug mode, the receiver **only prints text to Serial** â€” it does NOT send actual binary VESC commands. The `sendVescUartCommand()` function does this:

```cpp
// Debug mode â†’ prints human-readable text
#if defined(ARDUINO) && defined(RECEIVER_DEBUG_MODE)
    Serial.println("[DEBUG] ESC Current: 25000 mA (DRIVE)");  // â† text, not binary!

// Release mode â†’ sends actual VESC binary packet  
#elif defined(ARDUINO)
    Serial.write(0x02);  // â† real VESC command
    Serial.write(payload, 5);
    ...
```

**To actually control the ESC, you need to flash the release build:**

```
pio run -e receiver_d1_mini -t upload
```

Then disconnect the receiver from USB and wire it to the ESC:
- Receiver **TX** â†’ ESC **RX**
- Receiver **RX** â†’ ESC **TX**  
- Receiver **GND** â†’ ESC **GND**

âš ï¸ Note: the D1 Mini shares the same Serial pins for USB and ESC, so you **can't debug and control the ESC at the same time**. Once you flash the release build and connect to the ESC, you're "flying blind" â€” but the ramp limiter and failsafe are protecting you.

Is that the issue, or did you already flash `receiver_d1_mini` (non-debug)?

1: # Flipsky FTESC Protocol Overhaul
2: 
3: Wow, great catch checking the documentation! The standard VESC firmware uses a completely different UART protocol than Flipsky's "FT" series firmware. Because you have a Flipsky FTESC, it simply ignores the standard VESC packets we were sending. 
4: 
5: Here is the plan to swap out the VESC protocol for the proprietary Flipsky FTESC protocol based on the PDF.
6: 
7: ## Proposed Changes
8: 
9: ### [MODIFY] [receiver_app.cpp](file:///c:/Users/thatw/Documents/Apollo-8/DashBoard/src/receiver/receiver_app.cpp)
10: 1. **Update Command IDs:**
11:    - Standard VESC: `COMM_SET_CURRENT` (6), `COMM_SET_CURRENT_BRAKE` (7)
12:    - Flipsky FTESC: `UART_SET_CURRENT` (4), `UART_SET_BRAKE_CURRENT` (6)
13: 
14: 2. **Rewrite `sendVescUartCommand` to `sendFlipskyUartCommand`:**
15:    - **Start Byte:** `0xAA` (instead of `0x02`)
16:    - **Payload Length:** `5` (1 byte for CMD, 4 bytes for DATA)
17:    - **Command + Data:** `0x04` or `0x06` followed by `int32_t` current in milliamps (High byte first)
18:    - **CRC:** Flipsky uses **Modbus CRC16**, not VESC's CRC16-CCITT. We will implement the `crc16()` function exactly as shown in their documentation to ensure compatibility.
19:    - **CRC Endianness:** High byte first, then Low byte.
20:    - **End Byte:** `0xDD` (instead of `0x03`)
21: 
22: ### [MODIFY] [main.cpp (uart_test)](file:///c:/Users/thatw/Documents/Apollo-8/DashBoard/src/uart_test/main.cpp)
23: 1. Apply the exact same protocol changes to the `uart_test` diagnostic sketch. This gives you a working bare-metal testing tool to verify the ESC spins up without needing the remote.
24: 
25: ## Verification Plan
26: 1. Flash `uart_test` to the D1 Mini.
27: 2. Hook up the TX/RX/GND wires to the ESC.
28: 3. Power on the ESC. The motor should slowly spin (5 Amps).
29: 4. If successful, flash `receiver_d1_mini` (release build) and test with the remote!
30:

1: // UART ESC Diagnostic â€” tests VESC commands directly, no ESP-NOW
2: // Sends a small motor current command and blinks LED to show activity
3: // Connect D1 Mini TX->ESC RX, RX->ESC TX, GND->GND
4: 
5: #include <Arduino.h>
6: 
7: #define LED_PIN 2  // D1 Mini built-in LED (active LOW)
8: 
9: // VESC Command IDs
10: #define VESC_COMM_SET_DUTY          5
11: #define VESC_COMM_SET_CURRENT       6
12: #define VESC_COMM_SET_CURRENT_BRAKE 7
13: 
14: // â”€â”€ TEST CONFIGURATION â”€â”€
15: // Uncomment ONE of these to choose which command to test:
16: #define TEST_COMM_SET_CURRENT    // Test COMM_SET_CURRENT (our new command)
17: // #define TEST_COMM_SET_DUTY    // Test COMM_SET_DUTY (old command, fallback)
18: 
19: #ifdef TEST_COMM_SET_CURRENT
20:   #define TEST_CMD_ID   VESC_COMM_SET_CURRENT
21:   #define TEST_VALUE    5000     // 5A in milliamps â€” gentle test spin
22:   #define TEST_NAME     "COMM_SET_CURRENT (5A)"
23: #else
24:   #define TEST_CMD_ID   VESC_COMM_SET_DUTY
25:   #define TEST_VALUE    15000    // 0.15 duty (15%) â€” gentle test spin
26:   #define TEST_NAME     "COMM_SET_DUTY (0.15)"
27: #endif
28: 
29: static void sendVescCommand(uint8_t cmd_id, int32_t value) {
30:     uint8_t payload[5];
31:     payload[0] = cmd_id;
32:     payload[1] = (value >> 24) & 0xFF;
33:     payload[2] = (value >> 16) & 0xFF;
34:     payload[3] = (value >>  8) & 0xFF;
35:     payload[4] =  value        & 0xFF;
36:     
37:     // CRC-16-CCITT
38:     uint16_t crc = 0;
39:     for (int i = 0; i < 5; i++) {
40:         crc = crc ^ (payload[i] << 8);
41:         for (int j = 0; j < 8; j++) {
42:             if (crc & 0x8000) crc = (crc << 1) ^ 0x1021;
43:             else crc <<= 1;
44:         }
45:     }
46:     
47:     // Send packet
48:     Serial.write(0x02);       // Start byte
49:     Serial.write((uint8_t)5); // Payload length
50:     Serial.write(payload, 5); // Payload
51:     Serial.write((uint8_t)((crc >> 8) & 0xFF)); // CRC high
52:     Serial.write((uint8_t)(crc & 0xFF));         // CRC low
53:     Serial.write(0x03);       // End byte
54: }
55: 
56: void setup() {
57:     Serial.begin(115200);
58:     pinMode(LED_PIN, OUTPUT);
59:     digitalWrite(LED_PIN, HIGH); // LED off (active LOW)
60:     
61:     // 3 fast blinks to show we booted

* Identified the precise framing bytes: Start-of-frame byte `0xAA` (short packet), End-of-frame byte `0xDD`, and the requirement of standard **Modbus CRC-16** calculated over the `INFO` block (`CMD + DATA`).
* Implemented an on-the-fly shift-register Modbus CRC-16 algorithm and verified using a scratch script that it produces results identical to the manufacturer's extensive lookup tables, saving precious microcontroller flash memory.

### 2. Standalone Testing Environment (`uart_test`)

* Mapped control commands correctly to Flipsky instructions: `UART_SET_CURRENT` (4) for drive control and `UART_SET_BRAKE_CURRENT` (6) for regenerative braking.
* Maintained all built-in safety features:
  * **75%/sec Ramp Limiter** for smooth acceleration.

The search for ""FTESC" "keep_live" OR "keep-live" OR "KEEP_LIVE" OR "keep alive" uart protocol" returned the following summary:
The term **"FTESC"** refers to a specific series of Electronic Speed Controllers (ESCs) produced by **Flipsky**[1][2], which are distinct from the open-source VESC (Vedder ESC) platform[2][3].

Regarding your query about a **"keep-alive"** or **"keep_live"** mechanism within the FTESC UART protocol, here is the context based on current technical information:

### Context of FTESC UART Communication
*   **Protocol Nature:** FTESC devices utilize UART for communication, primarily for parameter configuration, real-time monitoring, and control via the "Flipsky ESC Tool" (available for Android) and compatible Bluetooth modules (such as the FTESC-BLE)[2][3][4].
*   **"Keep-Alive" Functionality:** In many UART-based ESC protocols (including those derived from or similar to VESC standards), a "keep-alive" signal is often required to ensure safety. This mechanism ensures that if the controller stops receiving data packets from the remote/transmitter within a certain timeframe, it enters a "failsafe" mode (e.g., stopping the motor or reducing speed) to prevent a runaway vehicle[5][6].
*   **Implementation:** If you are developing custom firmware or an interface for an FTESC:
    *   **Data Packets:** The protocol typically relies on continuous streams of data packets for commands (e.g., speed, duty cycle)[6].
    *   **Timeout Monitoring:** The ESC firmware itself usually monitors the time elapsed between valid UART packets[5]. If the interval exceeds a predefined threshold (the "keep-alive" timeout), the ESC treats the connection as lost[5].
    *   **Baud Rate:** FTESC devices commonly use a baud rate of **115200** for UART communication[2][3].

83:     payload[3] = (value >>  8) & 0xFF;

88:

92:     Serial.write(payload, 5);

99:     (void)value;

=== Step 868 ===
CONTENT: I have carefully reviewed the extracted FTESC UART Protocol PDF, and I found the reason why the `uart_test` code is still not spinning the motor even with the heartbeat included. 

In the manual (Section 3.1.4 on Page 10), there is a crucial limitation for the `UART_SET_CURRENT (04H)` command that we are currently using:

> **Note : This command is only valid when the Input Signal Type is set to OFF.**

However, if you have your ESC configured in the Flipsky ESC Tool to **"UART Mode"** (which is typical for electric skateboards), it will completely ignore the `0x04` command. 

For the ESC to respond when the **Input Signal Type** is set to **UART Mode**, we must use a completely different command: `UART_CONTROL_AND_OBTAIN_DATA_ONCE (02H)` (Section 3.1.2 on Page 7). This command takes a 14-byte payload where the throttle/braking is mapped to a `0-1023` value (512 is neutral, >512 is driving, <512 is braking), and it includes flags for gears, directions, and lights.

**How would you like to proceed?**
1. **Option A (Quickest test):** Connect your ESC to the Flipsky ESC Tool on your PC, change the "Input Signal Type" to **"OFF"**, and re-run the `uart_test`. Our current code should instantly start working.
2. **Option B (Update Code for UART Mode):** If you prefer to keep the ESC configured in **"UART Mode"**, I can rewrite `uart_test` and your `receiver_app` to use the `0x02` command with the 14-byte payload mapping throttle to `0-1023`.

Let me know which setup you are using or if you'd like me to update the code to Option B right away!

=== Step 871 ===
CONTENT: For an electric skateboard, **Option B (using "UART Mode" and the `0x02` command)** is significantly safer and is the industry standard approach. Here is why:

### Why "UART Mode" is Safer:
1. **Built-in Safety Limits:** When the ESC is in UART Mode, it interprets your signal (0-1023) as a throttle percentage. It then applies all the safety configurations you set in the Flipsky ESC Tool (Max Motor Current, Max Battery Current, Temperature roll-offs, and soft braking curves). 
2. **Smooth Throttle Curves:** The ESC will handle smoothing out the acceleration and braking using its own tuned algorithms. If we send a sudden 100% throttle spike, the ESC will ramp it up safely based on its internal settings.
3. **Simpler Logic for Us:** We just need to map our joystick to a number between `0` and `1023` (where `512` is neutral/coasting, `< 512` is braking, and `> 512` is accelerating). We don't have to manually calculate raw Amps.

### Why "OFF Mode" is Riskier:
If we use "OFF", the ESC becomes a "dumb" motor driver. It blindly executes whatever raw current value (`0x04`) we send it. If our code has a bug and accidentally requests `80 Amps` while you are at a standstill, the ESC will immediately dump 80 Amps into the motor, which could easily throw you off the board. 

### Recommendation
I highly recommend we rewrite our UART communication to use **UART Mode (Command `0x02`)**. It provides an extra layer of safety because the ESC will filter our commands through its own safety limits. 

Shall I go ahead and update the `uart_test` code and our `receiver_app` to use the safer `0x02` UART Mode command?

-    Serial.write(payload, 5);      // Payload (CMD + DATA)
-    Serial.write((uint8_t)((crc >> 8) & 0xFF)); // CRC high (Big-Endian)
-    Serial.write((uint8_t)(crc & 0xFF));         // CRC low (Big-Endian)
+static void sendFlipskyUartModeCommand(uint16_t throttle_val) {
+    uint8_t payload[15]; // 1 byte CMD + 14 bytes DATA
+    payload[0] = FTESC_CMD_CONTROL; // 0x02
+    payload[1] = 0x00;   // D0: Reserved
+    payload[2] = 0x00;   // D1: Reserved
+    payload[3] = (throttle_val >> 8) & 0xFF; // D2: Throttle High
+    payload[4] = throttle_val & 0xFF;        // D3: Throttle Low
+    payload[5] = 0x00;   // D4: Enable Direction Switching
+    payload[6] = 0x00;   // D5: Motor Rotation Direction
+    payload[7] = 0x00;   // D6: Speed Limit Gear
+    payload[8] = 0x00;   // D7: Horn
+    payload[9] = 0x00;   // D8: Headlight
+    payload[10] = 0x00;  // D9: Brake Light
+    payload[11] = 0x00;  // D10: Enable Cruise
+    payload[12] = 0x00;  // D11: Cruise Mode
+    payload[13] = 0x00;  // D12: Enable Multi-mode
+    payload[14] = 0x00;  // D13: Multi-mode switching
+    
+    uint16_t crc = calculateCrc16(payload, 15);
+    
+    Serial.write(FTESC_STX);       // Start byte 0xAA
+    Serial.write((uint8_t)15);     // DLEN
+    Serial.write(payload, 15);     // Payload (CMD + DATA)
+    Serial.write((uint8_t)((crc >> 8) & 0xFF)); // CRC high
+    Serial.write((uint8_t)(crc & 0xFF));         // CRC low
     Serial.write(FTESC_ETX);       // End byte 0xDD
 }

     static uint32_t last_keep_alive = 0;

2: name: using-git-worktrees
3: description: Use when starting feature work that needs isolation from current workspace or before executing implementation plans - creates isolated git worktrees with smart directory selection and safety verification
4: ---
5: 
6: # Using Git Worktrees
7: 
8: ## Overview
9: 
10: Git worktrees create isolated workspaces sharing the same repository, allowing work on multiple branches simultaneously without switching.
11: 
12: **Core principle:** Systematic directory selection + safety verification = reliable isolation.
13: 
14: **Announce at start:** "I'm using the using-git-worktrees skill to set up an isolated workspace."
15: 
16: ## Directory Selection Process
17: 
18: Follow this priority order:
19: 
20: ### 1. Check Existing Directories
21: 
22: ```bash
23: # Check in priority order
24: ls -d .worktrees 2>/dev/null     # Preferred (hidden)
25: ls -d worktrees 2>/dev/null      # Alternative
26: ```
27: 
28: **If found:** Use that directory. If both exist, `.worktrees` wins.
29: 
30: ### 2. Check CLAUDE.md
31: 
32: ```bash
33: grep -i "worktree.*director" CLAUDE.md 2>/dev/null
34: ```
35: 
36: **If preference specified:** Use it without asking.
37: 
38: ### 3. Ask User
39: 
40: If no directory exists and no CLAUDE.md preference:
41: 
42: ```
43: No worktree directory found. Where should I create worktrees?
44: 
45: 1. .worktrees/ (project-local, hidden)
46: 2. ~/.config/superpowers/worktrees/<project-name>/ (global location)
47: 
48: Which would you prefer?
49: ```
50: 
51: ## Safety Verification
52: 
53: ### For Project-Local 
<truncated 3064 bytes>
- **Problem:** Creates inconsistency, violates project conventions
166: - **Fix:** Follow priority: existing > CLAUDE.md > ask
167: 
168: ### Proceeding with failing tests
169: 
170: - **Problem:** Can't distinguish new bugs from pre-existing issues
171: - **Fix:** Report failures, get explicit permission to proceed
172: 
173: ### Hardcoding setup commands
174: 
175: - **Problem:** Breaks on projects using different tools
176: - **Fix:** Auto-detect from project files (package.json, etc.)
177: 
178: ## Example Workflow
179: 
180: ```
181: You: I'm using the using-git-worktrees skill to set up an isolated workspace.
182: 
183: [Check .worktrees/ - exists]
184: [Verify ignored - git check-ignore confirms .worktrees/ is ignored]
185: [Create worktree: git worktree add .worktrees/auth -b feature/auth]
186: [Run npm install]
187: [Run npm test - 47 passing]
188: 
189: Worktree ready at /Users/jesse/myproject/.worktrees/auth
190: Tests passing (47 tests, 0 failures)
191: Ready to implement auth feature
192: ```
193: 
194: ## Red Flags
195: 
196: **Never:**
197: - Create worktree without verifying it's ignored (project-local)
198: - Skip baseline test verification

Two-phase workflow: **(1)** craft a well-structured task prompt with

crafting without delegation is incomplete.

## Artifact-Based Workflow

Maintain a **prompt draft artifact** (prompt_draft.md) throughout the
process. It serves as both a live display for the user and a step
tracker for you. **Create it immediately** with this scaffold:

```markdown
# Teamwork Project Prompt â€” Draft

> Status: Step 1 â€” Eliciting project idea
> Goal: Craft prompt â†’ get user approval â†’ delegate to teamwork_preview

[Project description â€” 1-2 sentences]

Working directory: [TBD]

## Requirements

### R1. [TBD]

### R2. [TBD]

## Acceptance Criteria

#
<truncated 8048 bytes>
ontrolled infrastructure, if needed]
...

## Acceptance Criteria

### [Criterion category]
- [ ] [Objective, checkable condition]
...
```

**Validation checklist:**

- [ ] No implementation hints unless explicitly requested by the user
- [ ] Every acceptance criterion is objectively checkable without
      human judgment
- [ ] Requirements scoped by user needs, not by what the agent "should" do
- [ ] Infrastructure constraints clearly state what's controlled and why
- [ ] A skilled engineer would NOT feel over-constrained
- [ ] An agent could NOT trivially self-certify a half-baked result

Present final prompt to user. Ask for approval.
Set artifact status to: Ready for launch â€” awaiting user approval.

Once approved â†’ execute the **Delegation Protocol** (final section).

## Anti-Patterns

| âŒ Anti-pattern | Why |
|----------------|-----|
| Pass artifact file path as prompt source | Artifact may change after launch; always copy text |
| Invoke the teamwork subagent before explicit user approval | User must confirm readiness |
| Skip creating the artifact | Artifact is the user's window into the prompt |
| Lose the draft on iteration | If user wants changes after Step 8, update and re-present |
| Add implementation hints by default | Narrows agent team's solution space. If user explicitly wants to constrain (e.g., "use Python"), include as a requirement but flag the trade-off |

## Iterate After First Run

Prompt crafting is iterative. If the first run falls short, tighten
    acceptance criteria or add better verification â€” prefer this over
    adding implementation hints. Re-run with the updated prompt.

## Delegation Protocol

When the user approves ("go", "looks good", "launch", "run it", or
    similar):

1. Extract the complete prompt text from prompt_draft.md.
2. Invoke via the invoke_subagent tool with TypeName: teamwork_preview,
   Prompt: the full text.
   (teamwork_preview is hidden from the subagents list but can be invoked.)

Set artifact status to: Launched.
</TEAMWORK>
</ADDITIONAL_METADATA>

## Artifact-Based Workflow

Maintain a **prompt draft artifact** (prompt_draft.md) throughout the
process. It serves as both a live display for the user and a step
tracker for you. **Create it immediately** with this scaffold:

```markdown
# Teamwork Project Prompt â€” Draft

> Status: Step 1 â€” Eliciting project idea
> Goal: Craft prompt â†’ get user approval â†’ delegate to teamwork_preview

[Project description â€” 1-2 sentences]

Working directory: [TBD]

## Requirements

### R1. [TBD]

### R2. [TBD]

## Acceptance Criteria

### [TBD]
- [ ] [TBD]

---
*Next: when approved â†’ delegate via invoke_subagent (see Delegation Protocol)*
```

Update the artifact after every step.

## Core Principles

| # | Principle | Rule |
|---|-----------|------|
| 1 | **Specify What, Not How** | Define requirements and acceptance criteria. Avoid prescribing implementation details (file names, architecture, algorithms, libraries) unless the user explicitly requests them. |
| 2 | **Objecti
<truncated 8128 bytes>
[ ] An agent could NOT trivially self-certify a half-baked result

Present final prompt to user. Ask for approval.
Set artifact status to: Ready for launch â€” awaiting user approval.

Once approved â†’ execute the **Delegation Protocol** (final section).

## Anti-Patterns

| âŒ Anti-pattern | Why |
|----------------|-----|
| Pass artifact file path as prompt source | Artifact may change after launch; always copy text |
| Invoke the teamwork subagent before explicit user approval | User must confirm readiness |
| Skip creating the artifact | Artifact is the user's window into the prompt |
| Lose the draft on iteration | If user wants changes after Step 8, update and re-present |
| Add implementation hints by default | Narrows agent team's solution space. If user explicitly wants to constrain (e.g., "use Python"), include as a requirement but flag the trade-off |

## Iterate After First Run

Prompt crafting is iterative. If the first run falls short, tighten
    acceptance criteria or add better verification â€” prefer this over
    adding implementation hints. Re-run with the updated prompt.

## Delegation Protocol

When the user approves ("go", "looks good", "launch", "run it", or
    similar):

1. Extract the complete prompt text from prompt_draft.md.
2. Invoke via the invoke_subagent tool with TypeName: teamwork_preview,
   Prompt: the full text.
   (teamwork_preview is hidden from the subagents list but can be invoked.)

Set artifact status to: Launched.
</TEAMWORK>
